<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="vector-borne-diseases">Vector-borne Diseases<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vector-borne-diseases" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The organisms which cause vector-borne diseases usually undergo part of their development inside the vectors themselves. The time taken by the disease-causing organism to develop inside the vector is called the extrinsic incubation period. Although the housefly is an insect that is known to carry bacteria and chlamydia, it is not considered a vector. This is because it is merely a mechanical transmitter of disease; the organisms do not develop inside its body.</p>
<p>The majority of vectors are insects with the mosquito being the most common. This is because the mosquito is responsible for transmitting more diseases than any other vector. Insect vectors usually acquire disease organisms by sucking blood from infected persons. They then transmit the infection by depositing infected faeces or body fluids in skin cracks or abrasions. Most vectors have quite specific breeding, feeding and attacking behaviour. They therefore only thrive in areas where suitable conditions exist for their survival. As a result, vector-borne diseases tend to be present all the time (endemic) in a given geographical area or population.</p>
<p>Many of the diseases transmitted by vectors can also become epidemic, especially when there are environmental or other changes leading to increased transmission. Some serious epidemics which have occurred in Africa have been as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Yellow fever: Ethiopia, Sudan, Nigeria, Ghana</li>
<li>Trypanosomiasis: Uganda</li>
<li>Kala Azar: Kenya, Sudan</li>
<li>Plague: Uganda, Kenya, Tanzania</li>
<li>Typhus fever: Burundi, Rwanda, Ethiopia</li>
</ul>
<p>When communicable diseases are present in animals all the time, such as the case of yellow fever in monkeys and plague in rats, the disease is said to be enzootic (epidemic in animals).</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 106 of 267
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Some Diseases and Their Vectors</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Disease</th><th>Causative Organism</th><th>Vector</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Malaria</td><td>Plasmodium falciparum</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Plasmodium ovale</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Plasmodium malariae</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Plasmodium vivax</td><td>Anopheles funestus (female mosquito)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Anopheles gambiae (female mosquito)</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Filariasis (elephantiasis)</td><td>Wuchereria bancrofti</td><td>Culex pipiens</td></tr><tr><td>Culex quinquefasciatus</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Anopheles spp (female mosquito)</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Yellow fever</td><td>Flavivirus</td><td>Aedes aegypti (mosquito)</td></tr><tr><td>Visceral Leishmaniasis (Kala Azar)</td><td>Leishmania donovani</td><td>Phlebotomus lutzomyia (Sandfly)</td></tr><tr><td>Trypanasomiasis (Sleeping sickness)</td><td>Trypanosoma gambiense</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Trypanosoma rhodesiense</td><td>Glossina palpalis</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Glossina morsitans (Tsetse fly)</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Onchocerciasis (River blindness)</td><td>Onchocerca volvulus</td><td>Simulium damnosum (black fly)</td></tr><tr><td>Plague</td><td>Yersinia pestis</td><td>Xenopsylla cheopis (Rat flea)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="malaria">Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Malaria is an acute infection of the blood caused by protozoa of the genus plasmodium. For a long time in Kenya, it was found mainly in humid low-lying areas of the coastal plains and the shores of Lake Victoria. However, these days it is also common in the highlands. Malaria is directly or indirectly responsible for much ill-health and death, especially of children.</p>
<p>The vector responsible for the transmission of malaria is the anopheline mosquito (anopheles gambiae and anopheles funestus), which thrive in humid, warm climates where water is available. The parasites develop properly in the mosquito in places where the mean temperature is 16 - 32°C. The cooler the environmental temperature the longer it takes for the parasites to develop in the mosquito. The parasite takes about 35 - 36 days to develop at a mean daily temperature of 16°C, and nine days when the mean daily temperature is 30°C or above. Mosquitoes have an average life span of two to four months.</p>
<p><strong>Remember: Treatment and prevention of malaria is one of the national public health 'high priority packages' in Kenya today.</strong></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="malaria-epidemiology">Malaria Epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#malaria-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Malaria is caused by the plasmodium (parasite) that is transmitted to human beings by the bite of the infected female anopheles mosquito. There are four plasmodium species and any of them can cause malaria. They are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Plasmodium falciparum</li>
<li>Plasmodium malariae</li>
<li>Plasmodium ovale</li>
<li>Plasmodium vivax</li>
</ul>
<p>In Kenya 98% of malaria is caused by plasmodium falciparum. The other 2% of the cases are caused by plasmodium malariae and plasmodium ovale. Malaria caused by plasmodium vivax is very rare. Malaria due to plasmodium falciparum is usually the most severe form of malaria and is called malignant malaria. The mortality rate due to malaria is highest in children under five years of age. In Kenya, malaria occurs in two patterns:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="endemic-malaria">Endemic Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#endemic-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Endemic malaria (also called 'stable malaria') is transmitted all the year round. This type of malaria is found around Lake Victoria and the coastal region of Kenya. Endemic malaria causes severe infection in children under five years of age and in pregnant mothers. The mortality rate is high among infected children. After repeated bites by infected mosquitoes older children and adults develop partial immunity to malaria.</p>
<p><strong>Remember: Endemic malaria is transmitted all the year and severely affects children under five years old and pregnant mothers.</strong></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="epidemic-malaria">Epidemic Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemic-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Epidemic malaria (also called 'unstable malaria') occurs seasonally and affects people of all ages. Seasonal malaria occurs in Machakos, Embu, Kitui, Tharaka and Marigat in Baringo.</p>
<p>Another form of epidemic malaria occurs in the highlands and those areas bordering endemic zones. This type of malaria is called highland malaria and is seen seasonally and affects all people severely. Highland malaria epidemics have had high mortality rates. The areas in Kenya which have been affected by highland malaria include Kisii, Nyamira, Kericho, Turkana and Narok.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 107 of 267
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Remember: Epidemic malaria occurs seasonally and affects people of all ages.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="transmission-and-life-cycle-of-malaria">Transmission and Life Cycle of Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#transmission-and-life-cycle-of-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Malaria parasites develop in two cycles: The first cycle takes place in the mosquito and the other cycle in the infected human being. The first cycle which takes place in the mosquito is called the sexual cycle, while that which takes place in the human being is called the asexual cycle. You will now examine each transmission cycle starting with the asexual cycle.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="asexual-cycle">Asexual Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#asexual-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The asexual cycle of transmission takes place in the body of the infected human being and starts when the infected female anopheles mosquito bites a person.</p>
<p>Upon biting humans, the infected female mosquito injects sporozoites via its proboscis into the blood stream (1). The sporozoites circulate in the blood for about one hour and then they enter the liver cells (2). In 10 - 14 days the sporozoites develop into liver schizonts while still in the liver (3). The liver schizonts later burst releasing large numbers of merozoites. The merozoites leave the liver and enter the blood stream (4) where they penetrate the red blood cells.</p>
<p>Inside the red blood cells, the merozoites develop into trophozoites. The trophozoites then develop into erythrocytic schizonts.</p>
<p>These erythrocytic schizonts burst releasing a shower of merozoites, which invade fresh erythrocytes (5). Some of the released erythrocytic merozoites form male and female gametocytes, which are sucked by the feeding mosquito.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260116-d79a72fd.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sexual-cycle">Sexual Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sexual-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The sexual cycle of the malaria parasite takes place in the body of the female anopheles mosquito. This cycle begins when the feeding mosquito sucks blood containing the male and female gametocytes. In the stomach of the mosquito, the male gametocytes mate with the female gametocytes. The fertilised gametocyte is called the ookinete (6). The ookinete stays in the stomach of a mosquito for about 12 - 18 hours after which it penetrates the stomach wall. Upon reaching the outer surface of the stomach wall, the ookinete changes into an oocyst (7). The oocysts grow rapidly and burst releasing large numbers of sporozoites into the body cavity of the mosquito. Many of the sporozoites move to the salivary glands of the mosquito (8) from where they are injected into the body of the next human being when the mosquito feeds.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260116-be80942a.jpeg" alt="img-6.jpeg">
The Disease Transmission Cycle of Malaria</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260116-1ce968af.jpeg" alt="img-7.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features-of-malaria">Clinical Features of Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features-of-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The incubation period of malaria is about 10 - 14 days after the infection. The symptoms appear once the invaded erythrocytes rupture to release new merozoites. This stimulates the body's immune system and the signs and symptoms of malaria then appear:</p>
<ul>
<li>Headache and dizziness</li>
<li>Joint pains</li>
<li>Backache</li>
<li>Fever and chills (high body temperature, rigors)</li>
<li>Nausea and vomiting</li>
<li>Diarrhoea</li>
<li>Excessive sweating</li>
<li>Jaundice</li>
<li>Enlargement of spleen</li>
<li>Convulsions</li>
<li>Anaemia</li>
</ul>
<p>A typical attack of malaria progresses through the following three stages:</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 109 of 267
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-cold-stage">The Cold Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-cold-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This stage starts suddenly and lasts for fifteen minutes to one hour. The patient's body temperature rises and they shiver. During this stage, the infected erythrocytes rupture releasing merozoites in the blood circulation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-hot-stage">The Hot Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-hot-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The hot stage last for two to six hours. The body temperature is high (40 - 41°C) with severe headache, nausea and vomiting. The skin is hot and dry.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-sweating-stage">The Sweating Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-sweating-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The fever drops rapidly and the patient sweats profusely. This stage last for two to four hours.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complications-of-malaria">Complications of Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications-of-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Severe malaria can cause serious complications and is life threatening.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="brain">Brain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#brain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Mental disturbance appearing as acute psychosis, meningitis-like symptoms and coma.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis-of-malaria">Diagnosis of Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis-of-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Diagnosis is made through:</p>
<ul>
<li>Clinical symptoms</li>
<li>Laboratory examination of thick and thin peripheral blood films/slides (smears) which demonstrate the parasites (Trophozoites)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-malaria">Management of Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The treatment of malaria depends on whether the disease is complicated malaria or uncomplicated malaria (severe malaria). Uncomplicated malaria is usually treated on an outpatient basis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-uncomplicated-malaria">Treatment of Uncomplicated Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-uncomplicated-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Dosage of tablets of sulfadoxine (500mg) plus pyramethamine (25mg) and paracetamol for all age groups.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Age Group</th><th>Weight Range</th><th>Total SP Tablets as a Single Dose</th><th>Paracetamol Tablets (maximum of four doses in 24 hours)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>&lt; 3months</td><td>&lt;5</td><td>¼</td><td>¼</td></tr><tr><td>3mo to 11 mo</td><td>5 to 9</td><td>½</td><td>¼</td></tr><tr><td>12mo to 3yr</td><td>10 to 14</td><td>¾</td><td>½</td></tr><tr><td>4yr to 6 yr</td><td>15 to 18</td><td>1</td><td>½</td></tr><tr><td>7 yr to 11yr</td><td>19 to 37</td><td>1 ½</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><td>12 yr to 15 yr</td><td>38 to 49</td><td>2 ½</td><td>1 ½</td></tr><tr><td>16 yr and above</td><td>50 and above</td><td>3</td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td>Dose per Kg body weight</td><td></td><td>25mg/kg of Sulfa component</td><td>Paracetamol 15mg/kg</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Patients presenting with coma, convulsions, respiratory distress, acute renal failure, jaundice, shock, hypoglycaemia, or acidosis due to malaria should be admitted into the ward for complicated malaria.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-complicated-malaria">Treatment of Complicated Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-complicated-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Age Group</th><th>Expected Weight (Kg)</th><th>Number of tablets per dose given eight hourly</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Quinine Sulphate 200mg</td><td>Quinine Bisulphate 300mg</td></tr><tr><td>&lt; 3months</td><td>&lt;5</td><td>¼</td><td>¼</td></tr><tr><td>3mo to 11 mo</td><td>5 to 9</td><td>½</td><td>½</td></tr><tr><td>12mo to 3yr</td><td>10 to 14</td><td>¾</td><td>¾</td></tr><tr><td>4yr to 5yr</td><td>15 to 18</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><td>6yr to 7yr</td><td>19 to 25</td><td>1 ¼</td><td>1 ¼</td></tr><tr><td>8yr to 12yr</td><td>26 to 37</td><td>1 ¾</td><td>1 ¾</td></tr><tr><td>13yr to 15yr</td><td>38 to 49</td><td>2 ¼</td><td>2 ¼</td></tr><tr><td>16 yr and above</td><td>50 and above</td><td>3</td><td>3</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Intravenous quinine in dextrose is used in severe complicated Malaria where the patient presents with vomiting and coma.</p>
<p>Remember: The treatment of malaria keeps changing depending on current research findings. Please check on the current treatment and adjust your notes accordingly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-and-control-of-malaria">Prevention and Control of Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-and-control-of-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chemoprophylaxis">Chemoprophylaxis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chemoprophylaxis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Antimalarial chemoprophylaxis using oral proguanil (PaludrineR) may be given according to the national guidelines for diagnosis, treatment and prevention of malaria for health workers. Individuals who will benefit from chemoprophylaxis include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Patients with leukaemia (lowered immunity)</li>
<li>Patients with sickle cell disease</li>
<li>Patients with tropical splenomegally</li>
<li>Non-immune visitors to malaria-endemic areas</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="intermittent-preventive-treatment-ipt">Intermittent Preventive Treatment (IPT)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intermittent-preventive-treatment-ipt" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>IPT is based on the assumption that the pregnant woman is infected with malaria. According to the Ministry of Health (MoH) guidelines, the drugs used for IPT are the ones that contain Sulfadoxine and Pyrimethamine (SP) such as FansidarR, MalaraxinR, FansidinR, MetakelfinR, OrodarR, and FalcidinR. The first single dose of three tablets of SP is given to the pregnant woman between 16 and 24 weeks of gestation; the second and last dose of three tablets of SP is given between 24 and 36 weeks of gestation. (MoH, 2002)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="vector-control">Vector Control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vector-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Actions to reduce mosquito-breeding areas include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Using insecticide-treated bed nets</li>
<li>Using mosquito screens in houses</li>
<li>Using chemical mosquito repellents</li>
<li>Cleaning drainages and water disposal systems</li>
<li>Clearing bushes and burying or burning rubbish heaps</li>
<li>Use of larvicides and insecticides</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="health-education">Health Education<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#health-education" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should encourage community members to seek early diagnosis and prompt treatment for malaria and to use insecticide treated bed nets every night.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="filariasis-elephantiasis">Filariasis (Elephantiasis)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#filariasis-elephantiasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a disfiguring disease caused by a tiny worm (nematode) called wuchereria bancrofti. It is mainly transmitted by mosquitoes; the culex quinquefasciatus found in heavily contaminated water especially in the urban areas and the culex pipiens and the anopheles mosquito in rural areas. These mosquitoes transmit the worm from person to person in the same way as malaria. The parasitic worm lives in the lymphatic system of the patient causing inflammation of the lymphatic vessels and lymph glands (lymphangitis, lymphadenitis), filarial fever, and eventually elephantiasis of the arms, legs and genitals. The disease is most frequent in the tropical coastal belts and the lake region.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mode-of-transmission">Mode of Transmission<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mode-of-transmission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The microfilariae ingested by the feeding mosquito exsheath in the stomach and become first stage larva. They then penetrate the mosquito stomach wall and migrate to the thorax muscles where they moult twice and develop into the infective stage.</p>
<p>Mature infective microfilariae migrate to the mouthparts of the mosquito. The extrinsic incubation period takes 10 -12 days.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260116-a7ba47d6.jpeg" alt="img-8.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">Clinical Features<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The presence of mature filarial worms in the lymphatic vessels triggers an inflammatory reaction in the walls of these vessels. When the worms die, more foreign proteins are released causing calcification of the lymphatic walls which eventually leads to obstruction of the flow of lymph fluid. If the obstruction of the lymph flow is extensive, chronic oedema develops in the affected areas of the body. Filariasis progresses through three stages.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 110 of 267
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 111 of 267
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="acute-phase">Acute Phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acute-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Fever</li>
<li>Eosinophilia</li>
<li>Enlarged lymph nodes</li>
<li>Inflamed lymph vessels (lymphangitis)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="sub-acute-phase">Sub Acute Phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sub-acute-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Fever</li>
<li>Eosinophilia (severe)</li>
<li>Attacks of dyspnoea (asthma-like)</li>
<li>Funiculitis (pain and swelling of the spermatic cord/s)</li>
<li>Epididymitis</li>
<li>Hydrocele</li>
<li>Lymphadenitis (tender lymph nodes)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chronic-phase">Chronic Phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chronic-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Lymphoedema</li>
<li>Elephantiasis</li>
<li>Chyluria</li>
<li>Hydrocele</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Fluid aspirated from swollen lymph glands or from hydrocele can be examined under a microscope to show the microfilariae.</li>
<li>Thick blood slides for microfilariae should be taken between 10:00pm and 2:00am. This is because microfilariae are not present in the peripheral blood during the day.</li>
<li>Blood slides for microfilariae may be taken 45 minutes after administration of a provocative dose of diethylcarbamazine 100mg.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The drug of choice for filariasis (adult worms and microfilariae) is diethylcarbamazine (DEC, hetrazan, benocide, notezine) 6mg/kg body weight daily in divided doses (150mg) eight hourly for 12 days for an adult. Diethylcarbamazine may be combined with levamisole. This combination kills microfilariae and reduces the parasite worm count in the body more rapidly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-and-control">Prevention and Control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-and-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The prevention and control of filiariasis includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Anti-mosquito measures; the same as those used for control and prevention of malaria</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Use of larvicides such as polystyrene powder in the pit latrine</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Reduction of human-mosquito contact including the use of insecticide treated bed nets and screening of houses</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="yellow-fever">Yellow Fever<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#yellow-fever" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Yellow fever is an acute viral disease transmitted to human being by the aedes aegypti mosquito. Yellow fever can spread rapidly, and case fatality rate may reach as high as 30% in non-immune populations. Yellow fever is a disease of forest monkeys (zoonoses) and is transmitted among them by the aedes africanus mosquito. Humans may be bitten outside the forest by mosquitoes which have acquired the disease from monkeys feeding on bananas and other agricultural plantations. In urban areas, yellow fever is transmitted by the aedes aegypti. Yellow fever is a disease of tropical African countries, especially in the rain forests.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mode-of-transmission">Mode of Transmission<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mode-of-transmission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The mosquito becomes infected after feeding on the blood of an infected monkey or person on the third day of fever. The incubation period takes 18 days at a daily temperature of 18°C and four days at 37°C. The cycle takes four days and once infected, the mosquito remains infected and infective for its entire life (about two to four months).</p>
<p>A person may also become infected with yellow fever through handling of blood from an infected individual in the first three days of the disease or handling infected monkeys in the early stages of viraemia. Laboratory staff may become infected when working on infected monkeys or infected mosquitoes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-picture">Clinical Picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The onset is sudden with the following signs and symptoms:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Fever</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Headache</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Backache</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nausea and vomiting</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A bleeding tendency (epistaxis, bleeding gums, haematemesis, malaena)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Liver cell necrosis (in severe illness) resulting in jaundice</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nephritis leading to albuminuria which may proceed to anuria and renal failure</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Yellow fever like most other viral haemorrhagic diseases has no specific drug for treatment. You only give supportive treatment and ensure that the patient is nursed in strict isolation using ordinary barrier nursing techniques. You also ensure that the patient has no further contact with the mosquitoes through the use of insecticide treated bed nets and ensuring that the room is well screened. This is to prevent further spread of the disease from the patient to other healthy people.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention-and-control">Prevention and Control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-and-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Administering yellow fever vaccine to all travellers coming from or going to yellow fever endemic areas.</li>
<li>Spraying aircraft coming in from yellow fever endemic areas with insecticides to kill imported mosquitoes, which may be infected.</li>
<li>Isolating all persons who have been in contact with the infected persons. Such individuals should be quarantined in screened houses for seven days.</li>
<li>Mass immunisation campaign for the community in areas infested with the aedes aegypti mosquito.</li>
<li>Spraying of larvicides in all possible mosquito breeding places including water holding plants.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="trypanosomiasis-sleeping-sickness">Trypanosomiasis (Sleeping Sickness)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#trypanosomiasis-sleeping-sickness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Trypanosomiasis is a tropical disease caused by protozoa called Trypanosoma brucei gambiense (Tbg) and Trypanosoma brucei rhodesiense (Tbr). The important reservoir of Tbr in the wild is the bushbuck. Trypanosomiasis affects both humans and cattle and is invariably fatal over varying periods of time if not treated. Trypanosoma brucei gambiense causes an acute, rapidly progressive illness with death from cardiac complications within several weeks or months. Reservoirs include antelope and pigs. Tbr is found in eastern Africa, now mostly in south-east Uganda.</p>
<p>Trypanosomiasis spreads very rapidly unless the source (the very first case) is identified early, isolated and treated properly. Trypanosomiasis is found in the same areas in Africa where yellow fever is found.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mode-of-transmission">Mode of Transmission<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mode-of-transmission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Trypanosomiasis is transmitted by tsetse flies which live in areas of wooded vegetation. Tsetse flies are usually not found in flat plains, closely cultivated areas or areas densely inhabited by people. There are two important types of tsetse flies known to transmit the disease to humans. There is glossina palpalis, a riverine type which breeds along rivers and lakes, and glossina morsitans the woodland type which lives away from water. glossina palpalis is the main vector of Tb gambiense, while glossina morsitans is the main vector for Tb rhodesiense and it prefers to bite cattle and game but will also bite humans. Of the two types of tsetse flies, glossina palpalis (which transmit Tb gambiense parasite) is the main vector in East Africa.</p>
<p>Tsetse flies become infected with sleeping sickness parasites when they take a blood meal from infected persons or animals. After a period of time, during which the trypanosomes undergo development changes, the fly is able to transmit the infection when it bites another susceptible animal or person.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260116-a2815e1a.jpeg" alt="img-9.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">Clinical Features<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There is considerable variation in the clinical picture of African trypanosomiasis (AT). Within a few days of a tsetse bite, fever develops due to the invasion of the blood stream by the trypanosomes. The incubation period between the tsetse bite and the onset of fever varies from as short as a few hours following the chance to several weeks. The early stages of trypanosomiasis are characterised by irregular episodes of fever with headaches, malaise, weight loss, muscle and joint pains, pruritus,</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>anaemia, skin rash, and deep hyperaesthesia (Karandel's sign).</p>
<p>The clinical features of trypanosomiasis depend on the infecting parasite as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Trypanosoma brucei gambiense (Tbg) infection causes a slow chronic sleeping sickness, resulting in death from the disease in several months or years. Pigs, dogs and antelopes are the reservoirs.</li>
<li>Trypanosoma brucei rhodesiense (Tbr) infection is acute and rapidly progressive unless prompt treatment is administered. The parasites damage the heart causing cardiac complications and death within several weeks or months. Pigs and antelopes are the reservoirs for Tb rhodesiense.</li>
</ul>
<p>Trypanosomiasis presents in the following three stages:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="primary-stage-chancre-stage">Primary Stage (chancre stage)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primary-stage-chancre-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Within a few days of the tsetse bite, a painful indurated erythematous nodule may appear at the site of the bite. This chancre may last for one to two weeks and then resolve spontaneously. The chancre occurs in 70% of cases in Europeans but is rare in Africans.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="blood-stage-systemic-illness">Blood Stage (systemic illness)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood-stage-systemic-illness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>During this stage, the trypanosomes spread to the blood, lymph and lymph nodes. There is fever, which does not follow any typical pattern but recurs at intervals of days or weeks. After the fever resolves, the patient develops anaemia, debilitation and general body weakness. The spleen becomes enlarged as well as the lymph nodes. The cervical lymph nodes especially of the lower back of neck</p>
<p>become visibly enlarged in 80% of patients - this is called Winterbottom's sign. The other signs and symptoms of trypanosomiasis include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Pruritic rash (beginning six to eight weeks after infection)</li>
<li>Hepatosplenomegaly</li>
<li>Poor appetite resulting in weight loss, debility, pitting oedema of face and lower legs</li>
<li>Impotence and menstrual irregularities</li>
<li>Heart failure</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cerebral-stage-sleeping-sickness-stage">Cerebral Stage (Sleeping sickness stage)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cerebral-stage-sleeping-sickness-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the terminal stage of trypanosomiasis. During this stage of the disease, the parasites invade the brain leading to mental deterioration and coma. Convulsions and localised signs such as hemiplegia and facial palsy may occur. Patients are very weak, they sleep during the day but are restless at night. As the disease progresses, the patients become severely ill and die if not treated.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Microscopic examination of the chancre fluid to demonstrate the trypanosomes</li>
<li>Examination of blood (buffy coat) for trypanosomes</li>
<li>Wet blood smear for microscopy</li>
<li>Thick blood smear for microscopy</li>
<li>Serological test (card agglutination test)</li>
<li>Lymph node aspiration (microscopy)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Day 1</th><th>Day 2</th><th>Day 3</th><th>Week 2</th><th>Week 3</th><th>Week 4</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>0.5ml</td><td>1.0ml</td><td>1.0ml</td><td>2.5ml*</td><td>3.5ml*</td><td>5.0ml*</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>important: The drugs used for the treatment of trypanosomes are highly toxic.</p>
<p>Common Side Effects of Trypanosomiasis Drugs</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Drug</th><th>Toxicity</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Suramin</td><td>Mild proteinuria, arthralgia, severe dermatitis, diarrhoea, nephritis</td></tr><tr><td>Pentamidine</td><td>Hypoglycaemia, nephritis, diabetes mellitus, injection abscess, collapse if injected intravenously</td></tr><tr><td>Melarsoprol</td><td>Jansch-herxheimer reaction, arsenical encephalopathy, mortality up to 100%</td></tr><tr><td>Difluoromethyl ornithine</td><td>Diarrhoea, abdominal pain</td></tr><tr><td>Nitrofurazone</td><td>Haemolysis, neuropathy</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 113 of 267
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Remember: The drugs used for the treatment of trypanosomes are highly toxic. As such the patient should be monitored carefully and the drugs administered very carefully.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention-and-control">Prevention and Control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-and-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following measures are effective in the prevention and control of sleeping sickness.</p>
<ul>
<li>Chemoprophylaxis; IM pentamidine 250mg single dose protects against Tb gambiense infection for six months in those working in endemic bush land areas such as wildlife personnel.</li>
<li>Bush clearing (which may harm the environment) and establishment of agricultural settlement will in the long run destroy tsetse fly breeding areas.</li>
<li>Use of baited flytraps which have an efficacy of 95% at reducing the tsetse fly population.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="schistosomiasis">Schistosomiasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#schistosomiasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This disease is commonly known as Bilharzia after Theodor Bilharz who discovered it in Cairo in 1861. The incidence of schistosomiasis is related to water use. Irrigation schemes or water projects for electricity provide the habitat for the snail vectors. Up to 75% of schistosomiasis is transmitted by infected humans while 25% is said to be transmitted by dogs, cows, rats, and baboons. In East Africa, there are two types of schistosomiasis, both of which are named after the causative parasite. They are schistosoma mansoni and schistosoma haematobium.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mode-of-transmission">Mode of Transmission<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mode-of-transmission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>S. mansoni is spread in infected stool while S. haematobium is spread in infected urine. When the schistosome eggs in the urine enter a body of water such as a lake, dam, rice paddy or pond, they hatch into free-swimming larvae called miracidia. The intermediate host for S. mansoni is a vector snail of the genus biomphalaria pfeifferi; while for the S. haematobium it is bulinus africanus. The miracidia, after being shed from the ovum, must enter the appropriate snail host within 24 hours or die.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="transmission-cycle-of-schistosomiasis">Transmission Cycle of Schistosomiasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#transmission-cycle-of-schistosomiasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Inside the body of the snail host, it takes the miracidia four to seven days to develop and multiply into numerous infective cercariae.</p>
<p>The snail sheds them in water where they can only live for 48 hours unless they infect a human. A human being becomes infected when they enter cercariae-infested water, such as when bathing, swimming, laundering, cultivating or fishing.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260116-7eb38bf6.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg">
The Disease Transmission Cycle of Schistosomiasis</p>
<p>The cercariae penetrate the skin and enter the bloodstream from where they are carried to the liver or bladder to develop into adult worms. Within four to six weeks, paired adults reach mesenteric and pelvic veins.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">Clinical Features<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Schistosomiasis as a disease develops in four stages, each of which is characterised by specific signs and symptoms.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="effects-of-late-stage-schistosomiasis">Effects of Late Stage Schistosomiasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#effects-of-late-stage-schistosomiasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>|  Urinary Bladder: | • Obstruction to and dilation of ureters leading to hydronephrosis which may cause kidney failure
• Pyelonephritis
• Bladder polyps
• Calcification of bladder
• Cancer of bladder  |
| --- | --- |
|  Liver: | • Portal vein fibrosis leading to portal hypertension
• Portal hypertension leading to oesophageal varices which may cause massive haematemesis
• Caput medusae and ascites
• Hepatomegally  |
|  Lungs: | • Pulmonary fibrosis leading to  |</p>

Bishop's Computers</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The diagnosis of schistosomiasis is confirmed by finding eggs in stools or urine during a microscopic examination. If this test is found to be negative, a colonic or urinary bladder biopsy can be done. Serological tests are also highly sensitive and yield specific results.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The main aim of treatment is to kill the adult worms and to stop their egg-laying activity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="drugs-used-in-the-oral-treatment-of-schistosomiasis">Drugs Used in the Oral Treatment of Schistosomiasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drugs-used-in-the-oral-treatment-of-schistosomiasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Type of drug</th><th>Dose</th><th>Contraindications</th><th>Side effects</th><th>Remarks</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Praziquantel (Biltricide, Distocide, Cysticide, Cesol, Cestox, Coestocide)</td><td>S. mansoni 30mg/kg twice in one day</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>S. haematobium 40mg/kg once (single dose)</td><td>None (drug of choice if liver diseased)</td><td>Mild giddiness</td><td>Expensive but equally effective for both types</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Oxaminoquine (Vansil)</td><td>S. mansoni 15mg/kg 12 hourly (twice daily) for 2 days</td><td>Epilepsy</td><td>Occasional drowsiness, dizziness Or psychosis</td><td>Not effective for S. haematobium</td></tr><tr><td>Metrophonate (Bilarcil)</td><td>S. haematobium 7.5-10 mg/kg body wt. given in 3 doses at intervals of 14 days; that is a total dose of 22.5 - 30mg/kg.</td><td>None</td><td>None if correct dose</td><td>Not effective for S. mansoni Cheap</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention-and-control">Prevention and Control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-and-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The prevention of schistosomiasis can be achieved through the following measures:</p>
<ul>
<li>Prevention of ova-containing urine and stool from reaching the water by:</li>
<li>Digging and using pit latrines</li>
<li>Safe water supply</li>
<li>Treating the infected persons</li>
<li>Attacking the intermediate host (the snail) using molluscicides such as copper sulphate which kills snails and their eggs.</li>
<li>Avoiding contact with infested water by using protective clothing when laundering, cultivating, swimming and wading. Bathing should be done at home (storing water at home for three days will kill the cercariae).</li>
<li>Conducting mass treatment campaigns for communities at risk using oral praziquantel, especially school-going children.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="leishmaniasis-kala-azar">Leishmaniasis (Kala Azar)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#leishmaniasis-kala-azar" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is an infection caused by a parasite of the leishmania group. The disease is also known as Kala Azar. There are three forms of leishmaniasis which are caused by different parasites.</p>
<p>The vector of leishmaniasis is the female sandfly (phlebotomus). The four types of sand flies are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Phlebotomus martini</li>
<li>Phlebotomus orientalis</li>
<li>Phlebotomus longipes</li>
<li>Phlebotomus pedifer</li>
</ul>
<p>In Kenya, the main vectors are phlebotomus martini which transmit the parasite leishmania donovani, responsible for visceral leishmaniasis. The species P. orientalis is common in Sudan while P. longipes and P. pedifer are commonly found in Ethiopian and Kenyan highlands. Together they transmit the parasite leishmania aethiopica which is responsible for cutaneous leishmaniasis.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Type of Leishmaniasis</th><th>Causitive Parasites</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Visceral</td><td>Leishmania donovani</td></tr><tr><td>Leishmania infantum</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Cutaneous</td><td>Leishmania tropica</td></tr><tr><td>Leishmania aethiopica</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Leishmania mexicana</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Mucocutaneous</td><td>Leishmania braziliensis</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mode-of-transmission">Mode of Transmission<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mode-of-transmission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The zoonotic hosts of leishmaniasis are mainly dogs and rodents, although in some parts of Kenya humans have become the reservoir as well as host. The parasites of leishmaniasis are transmitted when the sandfly bites an infected person and ingests amastigotes. On reaching the sandfly's stomach, the amastigotes change into promastigotes. After four to seven days, they migrate to the foregut where they develop into infective promastigotes. The infective promastigotes are then conveyed in the saliva of the sandfly during feeding.</p>
<p>During feeding, the sandfly tears the host's tissue to feed on blood and at the same time deposits infective promastigotes at that site. From here the promastigotes enter the bloodstream and into the macrophages. On entering the macrophages, the parasites escape detection by the body's defences and are spread to various body tissues.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="visceral-leishmaniasis">Visceral Leishmaniasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#visceral-leishmaniasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Visceral leishmaniasis is found in many areas of the North Eastern region of Kenya in Machakos, Kitui, Masinga, Tseikuru (Mwingi), Makueni, Kibwezi, and Wajir.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features-of-visceral-leishmaniasis">Clinical Features of Visceral Leishmaniasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features-of-visceral-leishmaniasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Visceral leishmaniasis is characterised by fever, splenomegally, hepatomegally accompanied by anaemia and weight loss. Visceral leishmaniasis has a rather long incubation period of four to ten months or longer, before definitive signs and symptoms manifest. Most of the patients (96%) are killed by secondary bacterial infections of the lesions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="visceral-leishmaniasis">Visceral Leishmaniasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#visceral-leishmaniasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Visceral leishmaniasis is found in many areas of the North Eastern region of Kenya in Machakos, Kitui, Masinga, Tseikuru (Mwingi), Makueni, Kibwezi, and Wajir.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features-of-visceral-leishmaniasis">Clinical Features of Visceral Leishmaniasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features-of-visceral-leishmaniasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Visceral leishmaniasis is characterised by fever, splenomegaly, hepatomegaly accompanied by anaemia and weight loss. Visceral leishmaniasis has a rather long incubation period of four to ten months or longer, before definitive signs and symptoms manifest. Most of the patients (96%) are killed by secondary bacterial infections of the lesions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cutaneous-leishmaniasis">Cutaneous Leishmaniasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cutaneous-leishmaniasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cutaneous leishmaniasis is found in West Pokot, Turkana, Baringo, Laikipia and Kerio valley. It is characterised by single or several painful chronic ulcers in those parts of the body exposed to sandfly bites, such as arms, legs or face. In the lower hotter areas of Kenya such as Baringo, the vector is the P. orientalis, while in the highlands of Kenya, the high altitude sandflies, P. longipes and P. pedifer are the vectors. Phlebotomus longipes bites human beings in their houses at night transmitting the parasite leishmania aethiopica, which is responsible for cutaneous leishmaniasis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-picture-of-cutaneous-leishmaniasis">Clinical Picture of Cutaneous Leishmaniasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-picture-of-cutaneous-leishmaniasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In about two to eight weeks following a bite from an infected sand fly, a small itchy papule appears at the site of the bite. Over several weeks, the papule grows in size expanding to form a single indolent ulcer or multiple ulcers. The disease may be mistaken for leprosy. There may be enlargement of the local lymph nodes. The lesions begin to heal spontaneously two to twelve months later. Cutaneous leishmaniasis does not spread to other body organs.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260116-15f426f2.jpeg" alt="img-11.jpeg"></p>
<p>Bishop's Computers

Bishop's Computers</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-of-cutaneous-leishmaniasis">Management of Cutaneous Leishmaniasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-cutaneous-leishmaniasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Small lesions may be treated surgically by curettage or by freezing, using liquid carbon dioxide or by infiltrating them with 1 - 2ml sodium stibogluconate. Large disfiguring or multiple skin lesions are treated in the same way as for visceral leishmaniasis using IV or IM sodium stibogluconate 20mg/kg daily for 20 - 30 days. The drug of choice for visceral leishmaniasis caused by leishmania aethiopica is IM pentamidine isothianate 3 - 4mg/kg once or twice a week.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mucocutaneous-leishmaniasis">Mucocutaneous Leishmaniasis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mucocutaneous-leishmaniasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
